Notre 

Notre Dame 
at a glance ... 

The University of Notre Dame, once an ob­scure school in the northern Indiana wilderness, has become in little more than a century a great institution of higher learning, recognized the world over for the high caliber of its teaching, research, and public service. 
Founded in 1842 by the Holy Cross Fathers, a French missionary community, Notre Dame is now governed by a predominantly lay Board of Trustees, one of several evolutionary changes which have kept the University among the 
pioneers in American Catholic higher education. The chairman of the Board of Trustees is Ed­mund A. Stephan, an alumnus of the University and a Chicago, III., attorney. The president of Notre Dame is the Rev. Theodore M. Hesburgh, C.S.C., who has served since 1952 as the Uni­versity's sixteenth president. 
Administration Building (36) 

The first president of the University, the Rev. Edward F. Sorin, C.S.C., whose statue stands at the beginning of the Administration Building 
mall, began his school for boys on the site of a log chapel. A replica of this log chapel, which served as a missionary outpost of the first priest ordained in the United States, is found today on campps, amidst more than 80 other campus structures, highlighted by the magnificent Notre Dame Memorial Library. 
Now well into its second century of service to the nation and the Church, the University consists of a Graduate School, the Notre Dame 
Law School (the oldest Catholic law school in the Unifed States), and four undergraduate col­leges:Arts and Letters,Science,Engineering,and 
Business Administration. Specialized units with­in the Universityinclude the Lobund Laboratory, an internationally known facility for radiation chemistry research; the Mediaeval Institute; the Jacques Maritain Center, the Center for the Study of Man in Contemporary Society; the Institute for Advanced Religious Studies; and the Center for Continuing Education. 
Father Hesburgh heads a faculty of 604 per­sons who hold advanced degrees from virtually every major university in the world. Laymen predominate on the faculty which numbers about 55 priests and religious, most of them members of the Congregation of Holy Cross. While Notre Dame is Catholic in its origin, tra­ditions, philosophy and atmosphere, other de­nominations have always been represented in its faculty and student body. 
Research is an important mission of every great university, and grants totaling more than $7,000,000 annually are testimony of the qual­
ity of Notre Dame's research programs. Lobund scientists are engaged in a variety of projects dealing with cancer, heart disease, nutrition and gerrnfree human surgery. The University's research in radiation chemistry, the most exfen­sive underway on any college or university campus, is centered in a new $2,200,000 Radi­ation Research Building erected by the U. S. Atomic Energy Commission. Other major areas 
of research include nuclear physics, polymer chemistry, international relations, aeronautical and metallurgical engineering and the social sciences. Expediting all of Notre Dame's re­search projects is a new Computing Center, built around a UN IVAC 1107. 
w 


Nieuwland Science Hall (52) 

A university exists primarily, of course, for its students. Notre Dame's 7,200 students, known both for their scholarship and their spirit, repre­sent every state of the Union and fifty-two foreign countries. In recent years graduating seniors have compiled an impressive record in nationwide fellowship competitions conducted by the Woodrow Wilson, Danforth and National Science Foundations. 
Approximately 4,700 students live on the campus, and nearly one-third of the University's buildings provide living, eating and recreation­al facilities for them. Most of the undergrad­uates live in Notre Dame's nineteen residence halls, each with its own chapel. They eat in the University's two large dining halls which serve approximately 16,000 meals daily. More than 1,200 students are members of the Army, Navy, and Air Force ROTC units on the campus. The Stepan Center and the La Fortune Student Cen­ter provide excellent facilities for dances, other extracurricular activities and special events. 

[Tinrmr 

Religion has never been "extracurricular" at Notre Dame. Sacred Heart Church, the Grotto of Our Lady of Lourdes and the many chapels and shrines all serve as reminders that religion and morality are the mark of the Notre Dame man. Students attending the University have an unparalleled opportunity for spiritual develop­ment. Courses in theology (not required of non-Catholic students) are included in the curricula of all the colleges. For it is Notre Dame's con­viction, today as always, that it is not enough for a University to develop professional com­petence in a young man. To it must be added a sense of moral and social responsibility which will fortify his family, professional and civic life. Notre Dame students are heavily commit­ted to the Church's social apostolate, which re­ceived renewed emphasis during the Second Vatican Council, During a recent school year some 500 students were involved in tutoring disadvantaged youngsters in the South Bend area, and the percentage of Notre Dame grad­uates who enter the Peace Corps has been re­markably high. 
Notre Dame's thousand acre campus, with its twin lakes and many wooded areas, is vir­
tually a self-sustaining city with its own post office, power plant, television and radio sta­tions and campus hotel. The Morris Inn. The University, with a full-time staff of 2,285, is one of the largest employers in the area. Its 
Grotto of Our Lady of Lourdes (28) 
current annual operating budget is about $3516 million. However, Notre Dame's endowment of $60 million is one of the smallest among the nation's major private universities. In September, 1967, Notre Dame launched 
an ambitious $52 million, five-year program designed to achieve development in depth of the University, particularly on the graduate 
level. Principle elements are the University's endowment for faculty salaries, its graduate and research programs, and its new campUs construction. The program is called "SUMMA: Notre Dame's Greatest Challenge.". 
O'Shaughnessy Hall of Liberal and Fine Arts (56) 
OF SPECIAL INTEREST 

Some of Notre Dame's buildings and facilities, because of their historic char­acter, recent construction or for other reasons, are of special interest fa visi­tors. Among them are: 
The Administration Building (36), surmounted by its famed golden dome and statue of Our Lady, was erected in 1879 after fire destroyed its predecessor. Also known as the Main Build­ing, it houses the office of the president and the other principal administrative offices of the University. Murals by Luigi Gregori depicting the life of Christopher Columbus can be seen along its first-floor corridor. It is a tradition that students do not use the front steps of the building until the weekend of their graduation. 
Sacred Heart Church (29) is the center of cam­pus religious life. Built in 1871, its Gothic spire encloses the oldest carillon in North America. Of particular beauty are its stained glass windows imported from LeMans in France, the baroque altar in Our Lady Chapel,

and 

and the "Pieta" by the late Prof Ivan Mestro­
vic of Notre Dame. The body of the late John Cardinal O'Hara, C.S C, former president of the University, is interred in a chapel to the left of the main altar. 
The Grotto of Our Lady of Lourdes (28) is a replica of the world-famous shrine at Lourdes in France. Completed in 1896, it is a center of devotion where students, faculty members and visitors pause to pray day and night The Confraternity of the Immaculate Conception of Our Lady of Lourdes, whose offices are lo­cated to the rear of the Grotto, distributes Lourdes water throughout the United States. 
The O'Shaughnessy Hall of Liberal and Fine Arts (56) is the home of Notre Dame's College of Arts and Letters. The stained glass windows in its Great Hall represent -the seven liberal arts of the ancients O'Shaughnessy Hall houses the University Gallery whose fine col­lection of painting and sculpture can be viewed from 1 to 5 p m daily The building, dedicated in 1953, is the gift of Mr I. A O'Shaughnessy and the late Mrs. O'Shaugh­nessy of St Paul, Minn. 
Nieuwland Science Hall (52) provides class­rooms and laboratories for Notre Dame's chem­istry, physics and mathematics departments Occupying a portion of the building is an "atom smasher" which is central to the Uni­versity's nuclear physics research. The build­ing, which was dedicated in 1953, honors the late Rev. Julius A. Nieuwland, C.S.C., who de­veloped the formula for synthetic rubber and founded THE AMERICAN MIDLAND NATURAL­IST which is published by the University. 
The Notre Dame Memorial Library (72), which opened in the fall of 1963, stands at the cen­ter of a new east campus quadrangle. The thir­teen-story building has acapacity of two million volumes and can seat nearly half of the Uni­versity's undergraduates at one time. The col­lege library occupies the first two floors, and the tower section of the building is devoted to graduate materials in the various academic disciplines and to several of Notre Dame's specialized research units. 
Lewis Hall (80), a residence hall for nuns study­ing for advanced degrees at Notre Dame, is the newest of the University's eighteen student residences. The four-story building, which ac­commodates approximately 140 nuns and in­cludes kitchen facilities and an enclosed court­yard, is a gift of the Frank J Lewis Founda­tion, Chicago, Illinois. 

The Hammes Notre Dame Bookstore (19), in addition to handling texts and other materials for students, has available campus souvenirs, 
religious articles and other items of interest to visitors. The bookstore's hours are 9 a m. to 5 p.m , Monday through Saturday Dedicated in 1955, the building is the gift of Mr. Romy Hammes, Kankakee, III. and the late Mrs. Hammes. 
The Center for Continuing Education (81), is the campus focal point for conferences, seminars and other specialized programs which provide an opportunity for scholars, businessmen and professional people to keep abreast with ad­vances in their respective fields. Constructed through a $1^2 million grant from the W. K. Kellogg Foundation, Battle Creek, Mich., the Center provides twenty-two seminar rooms, an auditorium, audio-visiual center, theater, 
and other facilities 
The University Dining Halls serve the student body and provide banquet facilities for up to one thousand guests. The North Dining Hall (60), which opened in 1957, provides six at­tractive dining rooms and basement storage space for forty carloads of food. A cafeteria, open to visitors, is located in the South Dining Hall (17). Its hours are 7 to 10:30 a.m., 11am . to 1:30 p.m., and 4.45 to 7 p.m. Snacks are 
available at The Huddle, in LaFortune Student Center, from 8 a m to 10 p.m. 
The Morris Inn (21) is an air-conditioned, 92­room hotel operated by the University at the entrance to the campus. It is the gift of the late Ernest M. Morris and Mrs. Morris of South Bend, Ind. Alumni and friends of Notre Dame are always welcome at The Morris Inn. Its dining room is open from 7 to 10:30 a.m., from 11:30 am .to 2p.m , and from 5:30 un­til 8.30 p m. 
The new Athletic and Convocation Center (79), with its twin arenas totaling more than 400,000 square feet, is the hub of campus sports com­petition. One arena has a seating capacity of 12,000 for basketball, convocations and other special events. The other includes an indoor track, a baseball infield, an ice skating rink and facilities for a variety of sports.

The information contained in this directory was compiled by the Personnel Office and the Student Government of the University of Notre Dame. Student ad­dresses are those that were turned in at registration or corrected at the Student Government Office before October 2, 1967. Persons wishing to make corrections should send their name, address and the correction to either the Student Govern­ment Office or Personnel Office, c/o Student-Staff -Faculty Directory. If suf­ficient corrections are received, a supplement to this Directory will be published later this year.

Operating 


Operating Instructions for Centrex 
ON CAMPUS -Dial the desired four-digit number as indicated in this directory. 
FROM OFF CAMPUS -Dial 284 plus the desired extension as listed in this directory. 
LOCAL -Dial ”9" for outside line, then dial number as listed in the South Bend-Mis­hawaka-Oseeola and Niles, Michigan direc­tory. 
LONG DISTANCE -On all long-distance calls, when the operator asks for your number, give her 284 and the number assigned to you. Do not give 284-6011. 
STATION-TO-STATION -Dial "9" for out­side line then dial "1” for direct distance dialing equipment then dial the area code and identification number of the distant party. 
PERSON-TO-PERSON -Dial "9" for outside line, then dial "0"for Indiana Bell operator, give her the area code, telephone number and the person you wish to contact. 
REPAIR SERVICE - if your phone is not work­ing properly, go to another nearby telephone, dial ”0" for your Notre Dame switchboard operator and report it to her. 
CONFERENCE CALLS can be placed from any station on campus by dialing "5Mfor the conference operator. It is possible to have 5 conferees on one conference call. 
TRANSFERRING INCOMING calls to another 
station on the campus can be accomplished by 
depressing the plunger completely down. Hold 
in this position for about 2 seconds -release 
andyouwill receive aring. Wait for the oper­
ator to answer and give her the station num­
ber or party where the call should be trans­
ferred. The above procedure must be done 
on the line that the incoming call originates. 
Do not go to another line and dial "0" for the 
operator. Transfer and incoming calls are 
answered in the sequence that they are re­
ceived. Do not hang up if the operator does 
not answer immediately. 

INFORMATION -For University numbers not listed in your Notre Dame Directory dial M0M for your information operator. For numbers not listed in the South Bend directory, dial 9 then "113" for an Indiana Bell operator. 
DIRECTORY CHANGES should be reported as they occur to the Personnel Department. Changes include additions, deletions, number changes, etc. 
TO REPORT AN EMERGENCY -Dial "0" 
operator and give location of accident name 
of injured person, if known, and extension 
of calling party. Calling party should stay 
on the line until the operator has made arrangements for the ambulance. 
Individuals in need of ambulance service for reason of illness should contact a private ambulance. Suggested ambulances are: 
Hickey 234-4155 Walsh-Bilancio 232-5853 
TO CALL YOU -Those off campus will need the entire seven-figure number of your phone. Please send it to them. Cards are provided without charge. Ifyou need more, they can be 
obtained from the Personnel Office. 
TO SPEED YOUR CALLS -Long-distance calls will go through twice as fast if you give the Indiana Bell operator the area code for the city and the number of the phone you are calling. Personal long-distance calls are to be made from campus phones only in an emergency and such calls must be chargedto your home telephone. 
SPECIAL INSTRUCTIONS TO STUDENTS ­Long Distance Collect calls incoming are not to be accepted from any source. A penalty of $5. 00 will be assessed by the University for each collect toll call accepted by a student. 
CREDIT CARDS will not be issued on dorm­itory telephones. Students desiring credit cards should obtain them through their parents' home telephone numbers.

(Lhe Retire jBamp Jlonor (Enncept 
The Honor Concept expresses in a single sentence the entire commitment of the Notre Dame student: 
"As a Notre Dame Student, I pledge honesty in all my academic workand will not tolerate dishonesty in my fellow students. " 
The Concept captures the meaning of the ideal of Honor, for it depends ultimately onthe individual's personal avowal of academic honesty, in face of all difficulties. Its development at the University is thus hindered by the lack of commitment to honesty on the part of some, and the lack of concern on the part of others. Yet the Concept, initiated by the students, faculty and administration together, is a common effort for a common good and its growth is the responsibility of all. 
A functioning Honor Concept can be not only a source of pride in itself to the University, but also a means to the attainment of ideals of individual and university life. Since it confronts the individual with the challenge to be honest with himseld and with others, it lays open the opportunity to attain a new level in human dignity. Since an individual student cannot realistically accept the value of honesty unless it is very nature, unites all students into a community bound toget­her by commitment to honesty. Finally, since the Honor Concept cannot function without the authority of the faculty andadministration in academic matters, nor without their support ofthe student effort, it implies acloser understand­ing, mutual trust, andmutual respect among the elements of the Notre Dame community. 
The Student Honor Council

Art Gajlery Calendar 
WEST GALLERY 
September 17-November 12, 1967 
RALPHROSENBORG. Agroup ofthe more recnt paintings of a contemporary American whose expressive color and quiet in­tensity have survived a decade of relative neglect. Many of these works were influenced by the artist's recent travels in Europe. 
November 19-January 7,1968 
DRAWINGS FROM THE GALERIA ARTE MEXICANO. The highly respected Senora Inez Amor has chosen from her gallery portfolios this group ofdrawings and watercolors byleading contemporary Mexican artists. 
January 14-March 10, 1968 
DR. JULIUS S. HELD SELECTS. On his recent visit to Notre Dame, the distinguished art historian selected this group of paintings, drawings and prints from the permanent collection. 
March 17-May 12, 1968 
THE ERNST ANSPACH COLLECTION. This is Notre Dame's 
first full scale showing of African primitive art from asingle 
collection. The collection was recently honored by a special 
exhibition in the Museum ofPrimitive Arts in New York. 
May 19-June 30, 1968 
SCULPTURE FROM INDIA, THE WILLIAM WOLFF COLLEC­
TION. Another grouping of non-European objects, which 
should lend variety and freshness of approach to the concepts 
ofart better knownto us. 
THE PRINTS OF GUADALUPE POSADA. Enrique Echeverria, 
former artist-in-residence at Notre Dame, has selected for 
us this group of prints by Posada from the Galeria Nacional. 
The craftsman whom Jean Chariot has called "Printmaker of 
the Mexican People, " Posada was a vital influence upon 
muralist Jose Clemente Orozco. 
July 8-July 29, 1968 

MEAD CORPORATION COLLECTS. Approximately forty 
paintings by contemporaryAmerican Artists are featured in 
this collection from the Mead Corporation. 
EAST GALLERY 
September 17-December 31, 1967 

PORTRAITS FROM THE PERMANENT COLLECTION. Visit­ing scholars frequently comment on the quality fo the portraits in the Notre Dame holdings, for both study and enjoyment. This is a group of the finest that date from the 15th through the 20th coptury from America, Holland, Flanders, England, Spain, France, and Italy. 
September 17-October 29, 1967 
REVEREND JAMES F. FLANIGAN, C. S. C. A one-man show­ing of sculpture and drawings by a member of the university faculty in the Department of Art, this exhibit includes several works which have won prizes for merit. 
November 5-December 31, 1967 
THE WALTER R. BEARDSLEY COLLECTION. This show of modern paintings and sculpture is a loan to the university by adistinguished Elkhart collector. Featured willbe works by Chagall, Ernst and Rodin. 
January 7-February 25,1968 
A HISTORY OF SCULPTURE FROM THE PERMANENT COL_ LECTION. Viewing sculpture from Primitive to Modern times brings out fascinating similarities and contrasts. Over two-thousand years of creative production and culture are repre­sented here. 
SIX CENTURIES OF ITALIAN ART. From one of the strongest areas in the permanent collection, the Art Gallery selects paintings, drawings, prints and sculpture to demonstrate the development in Italian art from the 14th through the 19th centuries. 
March 3-April 21, 1968 
FIFTEEN DRAWINGS FROM MEXICAN PROCESSIONAL FIGURES. This group of sensitive and expressive drawings by John Guerin is based on polychrome figures which are carried through many Mexican towns dureing Easter Week. 
REVEREND ANTHONY J. LAUCK, C. S. C. A one-man show­
ing of sculpture and drawings by the Director of the Univer­
sityArt Gallery. The exhibition willfocus upon the sculptor's 
most recent works resulting from his studies last summer. 
March10-April 28, 1968 
GOOD AND BAD ANGELS. ROSENWALD COLLECTION OF PRINTS. Fify interesting visual concepts of the supernatural beings by artists from five centuries and seven countries, in­cluding Durer, Cranach, Burgkmair, Rembrandt, Schongauer, Redon and Baskin. Throughthe kindness ofMr. Lessing J. Rosenwald and the National Gallery of Art in Washington, these prints will be loaned to Notre Dame. 
May 5-June 9, 1968 

STUDENT ART SHOW. This selection of drawings, designs, 
prints, paintings and sculpture is a cross section of work by 
Notre Dame Art Students during the 1967-68 schoolyear.
